colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u00a0</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong> All 58 CASSOWARY members and their general properties, with <em>D</em><sub>off</sub><sup>BGG</sup> indicating the BGG offset from the lensing center of mass. Since both Jones et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>) and Dariush et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib5\">2010</a>) criteria were used to determine fossil status, we include the magnitude gap in the <em>r</em> band between the first- and second-rank galaxies (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub>) and between the first- and fourth-rank galaxies (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>14</sub>) within 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. The <em>P</em><sub>J/D</sub> and <em>F</em><sub>J/D</sub> columns were added to differentiate between Jones(J)/Dariush(D) progenitors or fossils, respectively. Entries in boldface indicate optical fossil status being reached either now or after merging is completed. Dashes under the merged column indicate all galaxies within 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> merging into one BGG by <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00. <em>t</em><sub>merge</sub> indicates the expected merger timescale from Kitzbichler &amp; White's (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib19\">2008</a>) relation until fossil status is achieved.</p>\n<a name=\"apjaab430t2fna\" id=\"apjaab430t2fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>No spectra available for source galaxy; lensing confirmation needed.\n<a name=\"apjaab430t2fnb\" id=\"apjaab430t2fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>Double nucleus detected in archival <em>HST</em> imaging negating fossil status until merging is finished.\n<a name=\"apjaab430t2fnc\" id=\"apjaab430t2fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup><em>Gemini</em> GMOS data from Grillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib10\">2013</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaab430t2fnd\" id=\"apjaab430t2fnd\"></a><sup> d</sup>The geometry of CSWA 36 prevents the arc from being easily fit, and thus no offset was measured.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/suppdata/apjaab430t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aab430\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/suppdata/apjaab430t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div><h3 id=\"apjaab430s2-3\" name=\"apjaab430s2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Luminosity Functions</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Once all 58 CASSOWARY catalog members were sorted based on their fossil status, we converted galaxy apparent <em>r</em>-band magnitudes (<em>m</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>) into absolute magnitudes (<em>M</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>) using</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaab430eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{r}={m}_{r}-25-5\\mathrm{log}\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{L}}{1\\,\\mathrm{Mpc}}\\right)-{K}_{r}-0.85z,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>D</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> is the luminosity distance to the group, <em>K</em><sub><em>r</em></sub> is the <em>r</em>-band <em>K</em>-correction, and the last term is the stellar evolutionary correction involving the group's redshift (Roche et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib27\">2009</a>). We then generated three average luminosity functions (one for fossils, one for progenitors, and one for normal groups) using all the member galaxies for each category. Due to the low galaxy count in the brightest bins of the average luminosity functions, errors in galaxy count were handled using Poisson statistics with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn23.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma \\approx 1+{(n+0.75)}^{\\tfrac{1}{2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma \\approx 1+{(n+0.75)}^{\\tfrac{1}{2}}</script></span></span>, where <em>n</em> is the number of member galaxies within the luminosity bin (Gehrels <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib7\">1986</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaab430s3\" name=\"apjaab430s3\">3.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaab430s3-1\" name=\"apjaab430s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0CASSOWARY Strong-lensing Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Of the 58 CASSOWARY members, it was found that six are most likely large, lone ellipticals (possessing an <em>N</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a05) that happen to act as strong gravitational lenses and were not included in any luminosity functions or fossil/progenitor fractions. Of the remaining 52 strong-lensing systems, we found that 13.5%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.8% are<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaab430fn3\">12</a></sup>\n Jones fossils (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub> \u2265 2.0) and 17.3%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.6% are Dariush fossils (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>14</sub>\u00a0\u2265\u00a02.5), consistent with the expected 8%\u201320% fossil system rate for randomly selected groups within <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.2 (Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>). We found that 23.1%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.5% of the CASSOWARY systems are Jones fossil progenitors and 28.9%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.5% are Dariush fossil progenitors. This higher rate of fossil progenitors in the CASSOWARY sample is not surprising considering that Kanagusuku et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib15\">2016</a>) found that in the Millennium Simulation systems that were fossils at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 finished forming their BGG (creating the required \u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub>/\u0394<em>m</em><sub>14</sub> <em>r</em>-band magnitude gap) between 0.3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.6 on average. Since the average redshift of the CASSOWARY members is <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.4, we expect to see a collection of near-fossil systems, as we are seeing analogs to today's fossil systems in midcannibalization of their <em>L</em>* members. An interesting thing to note is that if one assumes that <em>all</em> the CASSOWARY progenitors and fossils become/stay fossils, one arrives at a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 Jones fossil percentage of 36.6%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.4% and a Dariush percentage of 46.1%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.3%; this implies that we should see far more nearby fossil systems than we currently do. One explanation why we do not see such an overabundance of nearby fossils is that fossils are transitory in nature, and the look-back time is long enough to allow some bright galaxies to fall within half of the fossil's virial radius, thereby breaking the fossil's status. Another explanation is that in the CASSOWARY strong-lensing sample we are seeing a subset of systems that are more likely to be fossil systems; these two hypotheses will be explored further in the nonlensing control sample section.</p><p>To better determine whether we are truly seeing the progenitors of today's fossil systems, we contrasted the galaxy luminosity functions of each category against one another, as fossil system luminosity functions show a clear deficit in <em>L</em>* members when compared to comparable-sized normal groups and clusters (Gozaliasl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib8\">2014</a>). Fossil progenitors might be expected to be a transitional step between the two extremes, losing <em>L</em>* galaxies as they are consumed by the BCGs. We created three average cumulative galaxy luminosity functions (a fossil, progenitor, and normal function) from the CASSOWARY lensing sample to ensure that we were comparing strong-lensing systems to other strong-lensing systems.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaab430fn4\">13</a></sup>\n Due to the large amount of overlap between the two prevailing fossil criteria (Jones/Dariush) in this sample, the cumulative luminosity functions combined both Jones and Dariush fossils/progenitors to minimize errors. It is important to note that the poor fitting at the bright end is due to the \"BCG bump,\" a known artifact of galaxy mergers in the centers of clusters and groups (Hansen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib11\">2005</a>). Excluding the BGGs, we found that overall the lensing fossil and normal population fits are nearly identical. However, when the BGGs are introduced into the data set, we found that the fossil luminosity function greatly diverged from the normal luminosity function at the bright end, as expected (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f3\">3</a>). The progenitor luminosity function matched the normal function at the faint end. However, the progenitor function fell between the normal and fossil functions at the bright end, suggesting that fossil progenitors are currently losing their intermediate members while gaining very bright members, thereby moving the groups closer to fossil status. This supports the notion that fossil systems form their massive BCGs via cannibalization of intermediate-mass member galaxies. SDSS images of the inner regions of groups classified as fossil progenitors also very often show an extremely crowded environment near the BCG, further supporting this mechanism of fossil formation (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f6\">6</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaab430f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaab430f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Left: cumulative luminosity functions of CASSOWARY systems separated by classification\u2014normal, progenitor, and fossil. The curves represent the best-fit Schechter function found using least-squares fitting for each population excluding the BGGs. The progenitor function falls between the normal and fossil functions at the bright end (<em>L</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010<sup>11</sup> <em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub>), supporting the idea that fossil progenitors are a bridge between normal and fossil systems. Right: same luminosity functions binned by inner- and outer-half virial radii. The inner regions exaggerate differences between the three populations, which may be due to frequent mergers/interactions of members. The outer regions show little statistical difference from one another; however, as a whole, the outer members possess more bright galaxies than the best-fit model would suggest. Error bars are at 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaab430f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadFigure/figure/apjaab430f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaab430f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaab430f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We also find that while many CASSOWARY systems visually appear to be compact groups, none reach the compactness criteria of a <em>\u03bc</em><sub><em>G</em></sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a026.0 surface brightness in the smallest circle that includes at least three galaxies within 3 mag of the BGG (Hickson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib12\">1982</a>), making none of our systems compact groups. This supports the findings of Farhang et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib6\">2017</a>) in the Millennium Simulation, where only 3%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02% of fossil systems were simultaneously compact groups. The fact that none of the 12 Jones progenitors in our 0.2\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.7 sample were compact groups contrasts their estimates of 36%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02% of fossil progenitors being identified as a compact group between 0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a01.0. We hesitate to place our own numerical constraints on this fraction, as our sample varies greatly from theirs and we believe it is not large enough to show any meaningful correlations on its own.</p><p>Much work has been done investigating the global deficit in intermediate-luminosity members and the value/evolution of the faint-end slope of the fossil luminosity function, finding that the deficit in <em>L</em>* members is likely due to cannibalization by the BCG and the faint-end slope is consistent with normal groups (Lieder et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib21\">2013</a>; Gozaliasl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib8\">2014</a>; Zarattini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib40\">2015</a>). However, little work has gone toward investigating fossil populations in different radial bins, where initial group conditions could still be encoded (particularly in the outer regions). Binning the average luminosity functions into inner (<em>r</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>) and outer (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn24.gif\" alt=\"$0.5{R}_{200}\\lt r\\leqslant {R}_{200}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.5{R}_{200}\\lt r\\leqslant {R}_{200}</script></span></span>) regions reveals that this deficit in intermediate-mass galaxies/abundance of extremely bright galaxies in fossil progenitors and fossil systems is exaggerated for <em>r</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f3\">3</a>, right panel). This is likely due to the increased galaxy density near the center effectively speeding up the galaxy interaction rate; therefore, the central regions of fossil or near-fossil systems should show the most extreme differences from nonfossils. While the inner progenitor fit is nearly identical to the normal fit, when the BGGs are included in the histogram a clear difference can be seen, placing it firmly between the normal and fossil functions.</p><p>We quantified the statistical significance of these differences between data sets via a one-sided Kolmogorov\u2013Smirnov (K-S) test, which gives the probability that differing data sets come from the same cumulative distribution function. For 0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>r</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, even with the relative lack of data points, lensing fossil systems showed only a 0.84% chance of being identical to normal lensing systems. Due to a larger sample size, lensing progenitors also proved to be significantly different from normal lensing systems, with only a 0.01% chance of being identical within <em>r</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. Unfortunately, due to insufficient galaxy counts in the lensing fossil population, we were not able to find a significant difference between lensing fossils and progenitors. The outer-half virial radius galaxies showed no statistically significant differences between the populations, suggesting that most differences in galaxy populations for fossil systems exist near the center, where the most processing has occurred. However, all lensing systems exhibit an average ~2<em>\u03c3</em> deviation from a Schechter function in the outer 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> for galaxies brighter than <em>L</em>*, which is surprising. Since these galaxies are farther away from the center, one would expect them to be much less processed and therefore be better represented by the fits. Additionally, there are no BGGs in the outer regions to skew the data away from a Schechter function. While by no means definitive, this suggests that lensing systems may form differently from nonlensing systems of comparable mass.</p><p>A consequence of the CASSOWARY systems acting as strong lenses is that we have a convenient and independent way to locate the center of mass of the inner regions of each group, which can help shed light on the supposed relaxed nature of fossils. The difference between this lensing center of mass and the luminosity centroid of the BGG offers information regarding the age of the group, with large offsets implying a younger system (Raouf et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib26\">2014</a>). On average, we see normal, progenitor, and fossil system BGG offsets of 13.5\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.6 kpc, 14.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04.3 kpc, and 7.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.0 kpc, respectively. Immediately apparent is the smaller offset fossils have compared to other systems; this supports the idea of fossils being more relaxed (and possibly older) than nonfossils on average. Also noticeable is the high offset and wide spread in progenitor BGGs, which could be partially explained by their disturbed nature evident in images and range of fossil transition timescales. Since the progenitor BGG offset is consistent with the normal BGG offset, one cannot say on average that progenitors are older or younger than normal systems; a case-by-case analysis would be needed.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaab430s3-2\" name=\"apjaab430s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Nonlensing Control Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>All members of our sample exhibit strong gravitational arcs near the BGGs, implying a high central mass concentration for these systems. To see how/if the presence of strong gravitational arcs biases our fossil system and progenitor findings, we assembled a one-to-one random control sample of nonlensing groups from the Augmented maxBCG cluster catalog (Rykoff et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib28\">2012</a>), Clusters of galaxies in SDSS-III (Wen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib36\">2012</a>), and Richness of Galaxy Clusters (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib24\">2014</a>) catalogs. Control groups were selected to match (within 10%) each CASSOWARY group in both redshift and galaxy richness, and when multiple matches for control groups were found among the catalogs, the closest match was chosen. To increase the accuracy of this one-to-one comparison, we found two nonlensing matches for each CASSOWARY group.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaab430fn5\">14</a></sup>\n To ensure that our nonlensing control sample's halo properties matched as closely as possible to the CASSOWARY groups, we compared each control group's stellar luminosity against its lensing counterpart's; this can be used as a proxy for a system's stellar mass in <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u2013<em>M</em><sub>halo</sub> relations. The average scatter in <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> for a given <em>M</em><sub>halo</sub> is a factor of 1.4 (Behroozi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib1\">2010</a>); we measure a factor of 1.1 scatter in our sample. This means that we are limited by the scatter inherent in the relation and not our sample (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f4\">4</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaab430f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaab430f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Comparing the group stellar luminosities, as a proxy for stellar mass, of the nonlensing control groups vs. their lensing counterparts to see how well the average halo properties of each sample match. We find a best fit of <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn25.gif\" alt=\"${L}_{\\odot }^{\\mathrm{Non} \\mbox{-} \\mathrm{Lensing}}=1.1{L}_{\\odot }^{\\mathrm{Lensing}}-2.2\\times {10}^{10}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\odot }^{\\mathrm{Non} \\mbox{-} \\mathrm{Lensing}}=1.1{L}_{\\odot }^{\\mathrm{Lensing}}-2.2\\times {10}^{10}</script></span></span> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup>, which lies within the inherent scatter of the Behroozi et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib1\">2010</a>) halo relation.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaab430f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadFigure/figure/apjaab430f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaab430f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaab430f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Using the same photoZ and color cuts as the lensing sample, we found fossil percentages of 2.9%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.6% (Jones) and 13.6%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.2% (Dariush) and fossil progenitor percentages of 17.5%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.2% (Jones) and 25.2%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.1% (Dariush), showing that while being a strong gravitational lens does not significantly alter the Dariush fossil fraction, it does greatly improve the chances that a fossil will be a classic Jones fossil. The progenitor fraction is consistent between the control sample and lensing sample, suggesting that in general the progenitor fraction is not greatly affected by the presence of gravitational arcs. We note that while the nonlensing Dariush fossil fraction is consistent with previous findings (Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>; Gozaliasl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib8\">2014</a> for <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.6; Trevisan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib33\">2017</a>), our nonlensing Jones fossil fraction of 2.9%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.6% is below their estimates of 8%\u201320%, 22%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a06%, and ~10%, respectively. This could be partially due to the Gozaliasl et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib8\">2014</a>) \u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub>\u00a0\u2265\u00a01.7 criterion being less restrictive than the classical Jones et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>) \u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub>\u00a0\u2265\u00a02.0. However, we stress that their samples included nearby (<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.25, <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.6, and <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.07) groups; we are probing a different epoch (upward of 3 Gyr of available time for group evolution) by excluding the redshift range where most fossils are seen today. This, combined with the knowledge that most of today's fossils <em>began</em> BGG assembly between 0.3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.6 (Kanagusuku et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib15\">2016</a>), helps explain why we see fewer fully formed fossils in our control sample.</p><p>To see whether the lower fossil occurrence rate in our sample could be due to inadvertently including too many bright interloper galaxies (since spectroscopic data are lacking for these groups), we calculated the bright galaxy (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn26.gif\" alt=\"${L}_{\\odot }\\gt 0.4{L}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\odot }\\gt 0.4{L}^{* }</script></span></span>) overdensity within each CASSOWARY group compared to the surrounding regions. In nonfossil CASSOWARY groups, an overdensity of bright galaxies (sufficient to prevent that group from being a fossil) within 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> was confirmed at 13<em>\u03c3</em> confidence above the expected interloper value, indicating that our fossil fractions are reliable. The apparent fossil deficit could be accounted for owing to the redshift range of our sample (0.2\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.7). Since most fossils discovered lie near <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.1, and Kanagusuku et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib15\">2016</a>) found in the Millennium Simulation that most <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 fossils made the transition between 0.3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.6, there could be a lower fossil fraction in our samples.</p><p>Average galaxy luminosity functions were also generated for the nonlensing sample to compare against the lensing sample to see how strong lensing might affect a group's galaxy population (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f5\">5</a>). The nonlensing fossil and normal luminosity functions exhibit similar behavior to the lensing sample. Nonlensing fossils show a 0.01% chance of being identical to nonlensing normal and progenitor systems, confirming that on average fossil systems' inner regions house a different population of galaxies than nonfossils. Interestingly, nonlensing progenitors showed virtually no differences from nonlensing normal systems at any radii; this reinforces our hypothesis that the presence of a strong gravitational arc marks the most extreme examples of fossil formation at all stages. Also, the nonlensing progenitor fit falls between the other two in each radial bin. Reintroducing the BGGs maintains this in-between state for the nonlensing progenitors. Applying the K-S test to the nonlensing populations showed again that significant differences only appear within 0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>r</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaab430f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaab430f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Left: cumulative luminosity functions of our nonlensing control sample for all member galaxies. For nonlensing groups, differences in the bright end (while still visually apparent) are not as prominent as our lensing systems, supporting the existence of a strong-lensing bias. Right: same nonlensing luminosity functions binned by inner- and outer-half virial radii. The inner regions for nonlensing fossils and progenitors also show the excess of bright member galaxies when compared to normal groups, though, again, less pronounced than in our lensing sample. The outer regions show virtually no difference between progenitors and normal groups, with fossils only housing a few more bright galaxies. Error bars are at 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaab430f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadFigure/figure/apjaab430f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaab430f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaab430f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaab430s3-3\" name=\"apjaab430s3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Comparing Lensing versus Nonlensing Samples</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>A comparison of the lensed versus nonlensed fossil luminosity function revealed interesting distinctions; on average, lensing fossil systems lack intermediate-mass galaxies and house larger BGGs than their nonlensing counterparts (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f6\">6</a>). While the latter is not terribly surprising (as larger galaxies are more likely to act as good gravitational lenses), the former offers help explaining why the lensing sample has significantly more Jones fossils than the nonlensing sample; the lensing sample is the extreme case in fossil formation. In CASSOWARY fossils (and progenitors to a lesser extent), we are seeing elevated <em>L</em>* cannibalization, resulting in intermediate galaxy deficits and an overrepresentation of extremely bright galaxies. Comparing the nonlensing progenitor fit to its lensing counterpart reveals even sharper differences between nonlensing fossils and lensing fossils (top panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f6\">6</a>). On average, the lensing progenitors have fewer bright <em>L</em>* galaxies than the nonlensing progenitors. This could be an indication of a strong-lensing selection bias. Since the presence of strong lensing indicates a high mass concentration, lensing progenitors could have already had most of their <em>L</em>* members consumed by the BGGs.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaab430f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaab430f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Left: contrasting the best-fit Schechter functions, excluding the BGGs, of lensing/nonlensing samples at inner-half and full virial radius bins separated by fossil status. The CSWA strong-lensing fit is shown with a solid line, and the nonlensing control fit is represented by the dashed line, with the derived <em>L</em>* for each model marked. Right: galaxy member data taken from SDSS with BGGs included. Circles represent the lensing systems, and triangles mark the nonlensing systems. In each case, lensing groups show a deficit of intermediate-luminosity members and an excess in bright members, implying that lensing systems could be an example of the most extreme fossil systems, along with them being more likely to become fossils as opposed to similarly sized nonlensing systems.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaab430f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadFigure/figure/apjaab430f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaab430f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaab430f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Since Dariush fossils do not need such a large luminosity difference between the BGG and the next-ranked galaxies, the nonlensing control sample holds many more Dariush fossils than Jones fossils. The nonlensing control sample also indicates that while the presence of a strong gravitational arc does not strongly affect the likelihood of finding the progenitors to today's fossils around <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.4, it does appear to greatly increase the probability of locating Jones fossil systems. Applying the K-S test, this time to lensing versus nonlensing systems, again shows significant differences only within 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. For the inner regions, normal systems proved to be consistent between lensing and nonlensing systems. Progenitors, on the other hand, showed a 0.01% chance of being identical; such a striking result means that a strong-lensing bias may very well exist. Since lensing progenitors on average show different galaxy populations than nonlensing progenitors, it can be inferred that the same (if not more) can be said for lensing versus nonlensing fossils. Unfortunately, errors in bin count for both fossil populations kept us from arriving at any meaningful result; this can be remedied by increasing the sample size.</p><p>When one compares the best-fit Schechter functions of lensing versus nonlensing systems as a whole, an interesting distinction is found: at every radial bin, systems acting as strong gravitational lenses exhibit an intermediate-luminosity member deficit regardless of fossil status. While this is expected near the center of most groups (the act of forming the BGG consumes many of these galaxies, thereby shifting <em>L</em>* toward the faint end), this deficit supports the existence of a strong-lensing bias toward fossil-like systems. To test whether or not these lensing systems are preferentially selecting systems with different initial conditions from normal groups, thereby supporting the idea that some fossils are born differently than most systems, the outer regions must be probed to see whether the galaxy populations differ there as well. However, the overall lack of members in the outer-half virial radii of the systems made any conclusions statistically insignificant.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaab430s4\" name=\"apjaab430s4\">4.\u00a0Progenitor\u2014Fossil Properties, Time Lines, and the Longevity of Fossil Systems</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Since we have many progenitors in the CASSOWARY catalog with a wide range of merging timescales until transitioning into a fossil system (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaab430t2\">2</a>), we can form a rough time line of today's average fossil system's formation process from its beginning, through the cannibalization phase building the large BGG, and finally concluding with a fossil system housing a large BGG and possessing a deficit in bright <em>L</em>* galaxy members. To better illustrate the hypothesis of formation of a fossil system through the progenitor phase, we have assembled a collage of SDSS images from the CASSOWARY catalog (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaab430f7\">7</a>). We order them to simulate the building of a fossil BGG via cannibalization of <em>L</em>* members. Early in the progenitor phase, we expect there to be many bright galaxies present in the group and concentrated near the BGG, since dynamical friction has slowed the orbits of the largest galaxies and caused them to fall inward over the group's history. As time until fossil status is achieved shortens, more and more <em>L</em>* galaxies will merge, with the BGG subsequently shifting the galaxy luminosity function of the group toward a fainter population, leaving only one or two bright galaxies near the BGG. Once the last bright member merges with the BGG, a fossil system will form housing an elongated (possibly asymmetric) BGG. As the BGG begins to relax after the final major merger, it will eventually settle into a massive symmetric elliptical galaxy stereotypical of fossil systems. A follow-up study of progenitors is currently being done using <em>Chandra/Hubble Space Telescope </em>(<em>HST</em>) data to better see how the hot gas evolves alongside the stellar population as a group draws closer to the fossil threshold (L. Johnson et al. 2018, in preparation). We expect to see a correlation between a progenitor's X-ray properties and time until fossil status is achieved as well.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaab430f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaab430f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0SDSS collage of CASSOWARY systems at various stages in BGG formation, in time order from (a) to (h). Appearing in order from upper left to bottom right along with time until fossil formation is complete: normal group (a) CSWA 23, (b) CSWA 10 (3.9 Gyr), (c) CSWA 26 (2.4 Gyr), (d) CSWA 30 (2.0 Gyr), (e) CSWA 2 (0.9 Gyr), (f) CSWA 11 (double nuclei; &lt; 100 Myr), (g) CSWA 4 (young fossil with ongoing mergers), (h) CSWA 1 (relaxed fossil).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaab430f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadFigure/figure/apjaab430f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaab430f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/856/2/131/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaab430f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The high redshift of the CASSOWARY sample is beneficial for locating possible precursors to nearby fossil systems. However, the look-back time that allows large BGGs to form also allows new galaxies to fall within 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, potentially destroying a system's fossil status before <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00. To account for this chance as conservatively as possible, we define a \"danger zone\" for each system; this zone is the maximum projected distance from which a galaxy could free-fall inside 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> within the look-back time. The free-fall time is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaab430eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{t}_{{ff}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{\\pi }{2}\\displaystyle \\frac{{r}^{\\tfrac{3}{2}}}{\\sqrt{2G(M+m)}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where we took <em>r</em> to be the distance from the galaxy down to 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> for the group, as this is the threshold for a member to be considered in a system's fossil classification. In an accelerating universe, the ultimate mass of a galaxy cluster in the far future is around two times the virial mass (<em>M</em><sub>200</sub>) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 (Busha et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib3\">2005</a>). Since the density inside the virial radius is constant by definition, the virial radius will scale as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn27.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{200}^{1/3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{200}^{1/3}</script></span></span>, making the final radius ~1.25 times the current <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. The turnaround radius (the point beyond which matter near an overdensity of a certain mass will not collapse inward but expand) is defined as 2<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>, implying that the final turnaround radius will be ~2.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00. However, since our targets are at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00, their masses have grown between when we have observed then and now. Assuming a mass growth of a factor of four between then and now, that gives a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 virial radius of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn28.gif\" alt=\"$1.6{R}_{200}^{\\mathrm{obs}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">1.6{R}_{200}^{\\mathrm{obs}}</script></span></span> and a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 turnaround radius of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/856/2/131/Full/apjaab430ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"$3.2{R}_{200}^{\\mathrm{obs}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">3.2{R}_{200}^{\\mathrm{obs}}</script></span></span>. For this work, we adopt a turnaround radius of 3.0<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> and set this as the upper limit to our \"danger zone.\" All bright galaxies within the \"danger zone\" that passed our group inclusion criteria and were bright enough to violate either the Jones (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>12</sub> \u2265 2.0) or Dariush (\u0394<em>m</em><sub>14</sub>\u00a0\u2265\u00a02.5) fossil criteria were flagged. Groups with flagged galaxies within their \"danger zone\" may still be fossil progenitors; however, one cannot rule out the possibility that one or more of the flagged galaxies will eventually fall into the group potential. We found that out of the 28 strong-lensing fossil/progenitor systems in the CASSOWARY catalog, only two (CSWA 26 and CSWA 159) have no nearby galaxies bright enough to endanger their eventual fossil statuses, making these true fossil progenitors.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaab430fn6\">15</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>In simulations, the entire lifetime of fossil systems can be chronicled by observing when and if bright galaxies fall into the group. Observationally, it is more difficult, as we do not know the proper motions of all galaxies around the group. Spectroscopic redshifts of galaxies in and near the group can constrain the radial velocities; however, the tangential components remain unknown. This means that we cannot know for certain which bright galaxy outside 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub> will fall inward. Therefore, the result that only ~7% of 0.2\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.7 fossil/progenitor systems will stay fossils until <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00 is an extremely conservative estimate. Spectra of observed fossils and progenitors at higher redshifts have the potential to increase this estimate, as some bright galaxies will undoubtedly be eliminated, being identified as either foreground or background.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaab430s5\" name=\"apjaab430s5\">5.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The progenitors to today's fossil systems have been shown to exist in the universe and can be located. Kanagusuku et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib15\">2016</a>) found that in the Millennium Simulation most of today's fossils finished forming their BGGs between 0.3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.6, which also happens to be the optimal distance to see strong gravitational lensing. The discovery of the Cheshire Cat fossil group progenitor (CSWA 2) in the CASSOWARY catalog of strong-lensing events in SDSS prompted us to analyze the remaining 57 CASSOWARY members to see whether more progenitors could be located.</p><p>In this study of CASSOWARY strong-lensing systems, we find fossil rates of 13.5%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.8% and 17.3%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.6% for the Jones et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>) and Dariush et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib5\">2010</a>) criteria, respectively, which is consistent with the expected rate of 8%\u201320% of all groups being fossil systems (Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaab430bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaab430bib14\">2003</a>). This contrasts with our nonlensing control fossil rates of 2.9%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.6% and 13.6%\u00b1\u00a01.2%, indicating the presence of a strong-lensing bias toward classical (Jones) fossil formation. Our CASSOWARY progenitor rates of 23.1%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.5% and 28.9%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.5% (Jones and Dariush, respectively) are also elevated compared to our nonlensing progenitor rates of 17.5%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.2% and 25.2%\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.1%. Average galaxy luminosity functions for each class of system (normal, progenitor, and fossil systems) confirmed that fossil progenitors fall between the normal and fossil fits, indicating the formation of the <em>L</em>* galaxy deficit observed in fossil systems. For the CASSOWARY sample, the progenitor luminosity function showed a slight deficit of intermediate member galaxies, supporting the hypothesis that fossil BGGs are formed via cannibalization of their <em>L</em>* neighbors and that we are witnessing this process in some CASSOWARY fossil progenitors.</p><p>A control sample of nonlensing groups at similar redshifts, galaxy counts, and total stellar mass was compiled to compare against the CASSOWARY lensing systems to see how the conditions leading to a strong gravitational arc near the BGG could bias the sample. It was found that while being a strong gravitational lens slightly increases the odds of a group being a fossil progenitor, it greatly increases the odds that a group will be a Jones fossil, indicating the existence of a strong-lensing bias possibly linked to the initial formation of the lensing group. Comparing the cumulative galaxy luminosity functions of the nonlensing control sample to the CASSOWARY groups showed the nonlensing progenitor function agreeing more with the nonlensing normal groups rather than transitioning to the nonlensing fossil luminosity function. This could also be an indication of the strong-lensing bias preferentially selecting the most extreme examples of fossil formation (i.e., systems with the highest mass concentration, largest intermediate-mass galaxy deficit, largest BGGs). Additionally, we observe lensing systems possessing an average of 2<em>\u03c3</em> more bright galaxies than the best fit gives for galaxies outside 0.5<em>R</em><sub>200</sub>. This is not seen in the nonlensing systems, further supporting the existence of a strong-lensing bias toward classical (and possibly older) fossil-like systems.</p><p>Most fossil progenitors in this study seem to be in the process of forming a massive BGG via <em>L</em>* member cannibalization, making these likely progenitors of the observed cool-core fossil population seen today. We are engaged in further work on the topic of cool-core versus non-cool-core fossil progenitors using <em>Chandra</em> imaging of the hot gas of eight fossil progenitors in the CASSOWARY catalog at a range of stages in their evolution toward fossil status, from 100 Myr to 5 Gyr until sufficient merging has concluded, to establish the required <em>r</em>-band magnitude gap. Our goal is to observe the evolution of the hot gas component of a fossil progenitor and whether or not a cool core is present as the BGG forms (Johnson et al. 2018, in preparation).</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank William Keel, Jeremy Bailin, Yuanyuan Su MacLellan, Eddie Johnson, and Spring Johnson for useful conversations and constructive feedback. We also thank an anonymous referee for numerous helpful suggestions. This work was supported by <em>Chandra</em> grant GO6-17106X and <em>HST</em> grant HST-GO-14362.003-A.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn1\" id=\"apjaab430fn1\">10\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>It is important to note that it is logically possible for a BGG/BCG to be extremely efficient at turning gas into stars, essentially preventing any other large galaxies from forming in the group/cluster. Fossil systems formed via this channel would simply be \"born that way\"; however, our data cannot distinguish between this process and early dry mergers.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn2\" id=\"apjaab430fn2\">11\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://kcor.sai.msu.ru/\">http://kcor.sai.msu.ru/</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn3\" id=\"apjaab430fn3\">12\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Errors are reported at 1<em>\u03c3</em> confidence.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn4\" id=\"apjaab430fn4\">13\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In the cases of split identification (e.g., Jones fossil and Dariush progenitor), the Jones criterion was chosen for forming the luminosity functions since it is the most widely cited.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn5\" id=\"apjaab430fn5\">14\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Only one nonlensing match was able to be found for CSWA 31 and CSWA 102 owing to their high redshifts (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.683 and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.639) and poor member count (<em>N</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0=\u00a05).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaab430fn6\" id=\"apjaab430fn6\">15\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>It is interesting to note that both CSWA 26 and CSWA 159 are classified as Dariush fossils and Jones progenitors, implying that in both cases we are witnessing the formation of extremely massive BGGs compared to their respective group richnesses.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">Using Strong Gravitational Lensing to Identify Fossil Group Progenitors</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Lucas E. Peter Maksym</span><a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://orcid.org/0000-0002-2203-7889\" title=\"https://orcid.org/0000-0002-2203-7889\"><svg xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 256 256\" width=\"20\" class=\"orcid-logo\"><path class=\"st0\" d=\"M256 128c0 70.7-57.3 128-128 128C57.3 256 0 198.7 0 128 0 57.3 57.3 0 128 0 198.7 0 256 57.3 256 128z\"></path><path class=\"st1\" d=\"M86.3 186.2H70.9V79.1h15.4v48.4V186.2z\"></path><path class=\"st1\" d=\"M108.9 79.1h41.6c39.6 0 57 28.3 57 53.6 0 27.5-21.5 53.6-56.8 53.6h-41.8V79.1zM124.3 172.4h24.5c34.9 0 42.9-26.5 42.9-39.7 0-21.5-13.7-39.7-43.7-39.7h-23.7V172.4z\"></path><path class=\"st1\" d=\"M88.7 56.8c0 5.5-4.5 10.1-10.1 10.1 -5.6 0-10.1-4.6-10.1-10.1 0-5.6 4.5-10.1 10.1-10.1C84.2 46.7 88.7 51.3 88.7 56.8z\"></path></svg> https://orcid.org/0000-0002-2203-7889</a></span></p><p class=\"mb-05\"><span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Eleazar R. Carrasco</span><a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://orcid.org/0000-0002-7272-9234\" title=\"https://orcid.org/0000-0002-7272-9234\"><svg xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 256 256\" width=\"20\" class=\"orcid-logo\"><path class=\"st0\" d=\"M256 128c0 70.7-57.3 128-128 128C57.3 256 0 198.7 0 128 0 57.3 57.3 0 128 0 198.7
